<div id="review_590508972" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/590508972" />
    <a title="Adam Morel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16612967-adam-morel"><img alt="Adam Morel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1358760648p2/16612967.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/590508972?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam Morel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam Morel" href="/user/show/16612967-adam-morel">Adam Morel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16612967-adam-morel?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer590508972" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6209562643242555973">For over thirty years, I have avoided this story. A young man with little experience in the woods outside of a KOA campground need not soon revisit such a tale of horror and invasion. Still, the memories of the worst of it, the visual of it have stayed with me, however blurred. The albino boy with the banjo, the wildness of the river, and yes, the screams of Ned Beatty. But film has rarely if ever captured the dark beauty of nature or the hopelessness of true tragedy and so it became time to pla</span>
  <span id="freeText6209562643242555973" style="display:none">For over thirty years, I have avoided this story. A young man with little experience in the woods outside of a KOA campground need not soon revisit such a tale of horror and invasion. Still, the memories of the worst of it, the visual of it have stayed with me, however blurred. The albino boy with the banjo, the wildness of the river, and yes, the screams of Ned Beatty. But film has rarely if ever captured the dark beauty of nature or the hopelessness of true tragedy and so it became time to place the story into the frame for which it was painted by its author. Not the four corners of a television screen but the boundless, edgeless space of the imagination. <br /><br />James Dickey's novel is a haunted lyric, a trembling poem in homage to the blackness of the woods, the foaming malevolence of white water, the wretchedness of human depravity. His words are beautiful and terrible. <br /><br />As a man, I have learned to love the woods and the waters that flow through them. I have spent my share of time in the back country with men I have respected and loved in search of nothing more than that sought after by the men in Dickey's novel - decompression, detachment, deliverance. The strange otherness of the night, hanging between the trees in the same Appalachia is part of my own experience. And so I am frightened by this story and excited by it having bathed in the adrenaline that comes only from the place where courage and fear come to terms. <br /><br />I recommend this book to every weekend warrior who has depended on fire and fortitude and friendship to smooth the rugged wilderness into a place not to run from but to return to with all the exhilaration the human spirit is capable of achieving. <br /><br />This one, Mr. Dickey, goes on the top shelf.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6209562643242555973" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780440118688" href="/book/show/3316219-deliverance">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=modern-library-top-100-list">modern-library-top-100-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=survival">survival</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=adventure-fiction">adventure-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=__in-my-collection">__in-my-collection</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39016006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer657910436652424610">Some impressions of the book four months after having read it:<br/><br/>The book, unlike the film, is told completely from the perspective of ad agency studio artist Ed Gentry, and so there's a lot of stuff about his home, work, family and his lust for an artist's model that are completely missing from the film. The filter of having it all told by him, with his analysis, also is not part of the movie. Ed is a main character in the film, for sure, but quite diminished in the screen version.<br/><br/>The first encoun</span>
  <span id="freeText657910436652424610" style="display:none">Some impressions of the book four months after having read it:<br /><br />The book, unlike the film, is told completely from the perspective of ad agency studio artist Ed Gentry, and so there's a lot of stuff about his home, work, family and his lust for an artist's model that are completely missing from the film. The filter of having it all told by him, with his analysis, also is not part of the movie. Ed is a main character in the film, for sure, but quite diminished in the screen version.<br /><br />The first encounter with hillbillies as the men set out on their adventure is far less ominous in the book than in the film. "I like the way you wear that hat" and the reaction of the hillbilly in the film ("You don't know nuthin'") is a lot more eerie in the movie.<br /><br />Ed's climb up the hill to slay his invisible adversary is a tour de force of meticulous description in the book that is truncated (understandably) in the movie.<br /><br />Ed's following of his "prey" after striking him is more drawn out and interesting in the book.<br /><br />Both versions, book and film, are excellent. Each does honor to the other. But I was glad to have finally read the book after having seen the film so many times over the years. Highly recommended.<br /><br />---<br />eg/kr '08</span>

<div id="review_44604803" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44604803" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1567012-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1222574447p2/1567012.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44604803?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/1567012-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44604803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4142777515600201339">It's too bad that there is such an overwhelming popular culture taboo built around this book, because it is one hell of a good book. While I read it, I kept thinking to myself, "Why did none of my college professors ever force us to read this book?", all the while trying to ignore the simple fact that it would have been too much, too risque, too potentially offensive... and that, in a room full of English majors, who are generally not known for their robustness of constitution or even of knowing</span>
  <span id="freeText4142777515600201339" style="display:none">It's too bad that there is such an overwhelming popular culture taboo built around this book, because it is one hell of a good book. While I read it, I kept thinking to myself, "Why did none of my college professors ever force us to read this book?", all the while trying to ignore the simple fact that it would have been too much, too risque, too potentially offensive... and that, in a room full of English majors, who are generally not known for their robustness of constitution or even of knowing which gender they are - or are not - and thereby maintaining the long collegiate academic tradition of being not quite gay but not quite straight that is the perpetual lot of most people who read in order to be impressive conversationalists. What I'm saying is, is that the horror in this book - as in the Hollywood movie version of it - is a horror... yes, indeed, without exception. But the most well known part of this book is most definitely not the book.<br /><br />I personally think that the title belies how good this book is. Given over to Max Perkins, or someone of equal literary merit, something better could have been coaxed out I suppose. But whatever. Books. Covers. Titles. All amounts to about the same thing, doesn't it?<br /><br />The best thing about the entire novel is the interplay of solid, three-dimensional description and ethereal, recognizable, universal perception. The author knows how to capture moments that at first might seem too small and too personal to be of any merit, certainly of recording and probably of remembering, but then, in doing just that, he also makes the reader recognize them. There is recognition in the words, in their wrongness, that is accurate and true to the feel, if not to the letter, of thinking. The language is just something else, and when it is not, it is so ordinary that thinking it was made rather than simply recorded seems counterintuitive.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4142777515600201339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review699143426">

<div id="review_779083318" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/779083318" />
    <a title="Hester" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2151506-hester"><img alt="Hester" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565122995p2/2151506.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/779083318?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hester" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hester" href="/user/show/2151506-hester">Hester</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2151506-hester?shelf=from-book-to-screen">from-book-to-screen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2151506-hester?shelf=kindle-time">kindle-time</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2151506-hester?shelf=thats-so-trashy">thats-so-trashy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2151506-hester?shelf=it-stinks">it-stinks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2151506-hester?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2151506-hester?shelf=the-muted-colors-of-the-rainbow">the-muted-colors-of-the-rainbow</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer779083318" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13449146985993728056">The 1970s must have been a time when America was filled with enthusiasm for long boring descriptions of nature, anal rape by hillbillies, thinly veiled homoerotcisim, and of course Burt Reynolds. These are the only reasons I can think of as to why Deliverance was such a big deal.<br/><br/>The story starts off with Ed Gentry gushing about his friend Lewis and what an athletic man's man Lewis is. In other words, someone's got a big old crush on his BFF. Ed just lives for any scraps of approval that Lewis th</span>
  <span id="freeText13449146985993728056" style="display:none">The 1970s must have been a time when America was filled with enthusiasm for long boring descriptions of nature, anal rape by hillbillies, thinly veiled homoerotcisim, and of course Burt Reynolds. These are the only reasons I can think of as to why Deliverance was such a big deal.<br /><br />The story starts off with Ed Gentry gushing about his friend Lewis and what an athletic man's man Lewis is. In other words, someone's got a big old crush on his BFF. Ed just lives for any scraps of approval that Lewis throws his way, so much so he agrees to take a white water canoeing trip with him in the really backwards/redneck parts of Georgia before the state builds a dam or something like that. Ed doesn't really want to go, but he wants Lewis to think he's cool and adventurous and he wants to spend as much time with him as possible so he agrees. For some reason two other men agree to go, both are useless, but hey, every story needs its own Star Trek red shirts.<br /><br />First the reader must suffer through a long boring car ride with Lewis telling Ed how the hill folk are good honest and gentle people, much like Tolkien's Hobbits or some garbage like that. Lewis loves 'em, Ed is ascared of them and their backward ways. After getting to wherever they end up the men interact with the inbred hillbillies and find some to move theirs cars to another place. At this point Ed throws out a very useless thought, he wonders how things would have turned out if only they shook hands with these men. Why think this? Did I miss something? Were the mechanics the attackers?<br /><br />Boring canoe ride, boring night in tents, more boring canoeing then we get to the run in with the two hillbillies who change their lives forever. Drop your drawers lean over that log, sexual assault and screaming, drop to your knees boy then an arrow flies out of nowhere killing the one that was going to make Ed perform the delicate act of fellatio on him. The other one runs away. Back to more boring canoeing then wham one of the red shirts dies, Lewis breaks a leg and now Ed has to climb a cliff half the night so he can climb a tree to kill the one that got away with his bow and arrow. <br /><br />Through every step of their plan Ed turns to Lewis for praise and reassurance even though Lewis has a broken leg and is passed out, he can't stop bitching about the rape victim being worthless. Hey asshole, the guy was just anal raped by some gross mountain man and without lube, give him some slack. Ed finally passes the test that enters him into real manhood, he kills a guy and grievously injures himself. He's bleeding like a stuck pig but yet he doesn't die, it doesn't matter how much he bleeds and moves around, he just doesn't die, but I'm sure in real life he would've, he's so manly there's no need for him to get blood at the hospital. In the end, Ed gets what he's wanted all long, Lewis' unconditional approval and life long friendship and maybe a little bit more (wink, wink, nudge, nudge). <br /><br />In other words, what a horrible book about horrible people.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13449146985993728056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('28GLyNGz3uvsBXRDJEFWj4LUcy7zN8gEAfLeNsN2hE4lVVWCt7i0t5SVAskRZcjh7MMgJsbfpx/AcFKMHB9LEw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/31457.James_Dickey">About James Dickey</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/31457.James_Dickey"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1422858070p3/31457.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/31457.James_Dickey">James Dickey
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13963757699555948851">Dickey was born in Atlanta, Georgia. After serving as a pilot in the Second World War, he attended Vanderbilt University. Having earned an MA in 1950, Dickey returned to military duty in the Korean War, serving with the US Air Force. Upon return to civilian life Dickey taught at Rice University in Texas and then at the University of Florida. From 1955 to 1961, he worked for advertising agencies in</span>
  <span id="freeText13963757699555948851" style="display:none">Dickey was born in Atlanta, Georgia. After serving as a pilot in the Second World War, he attended Vanderbilt University. Having earned an MA in 1950, Dickey returned to military duty in the Korean War, serving with the US Air Force. Upon return to civilian life Dickey taught at Rice University in Texas and then at the University of Florida. From 1955 to 1961, he worked for advertising agencies in New York and Atlanta. After the publication of his first book, Into the Stone (Middletown, Conn., 1962), he left advertising and began teaching at various colleges and universities. He became poet-in-residence and Carolina Professor of English at the University of South Carolina.<br /><br />Dickey's third volume, Buckdancer's Choice (Middletown, 1965), won the prestigious National Book Award in Poetry. From 1966 to 1968 he served as poetry consultant to the Library of Congress. In 1977 Dickey read his poem 'The Strength of Fields' at President Carter's inauguration. The Hollywood film of his novel Deliverance (Boston, 1970) brought Dickey fame not normally enjoyed by poets.<br /><br />Dickey's poems are a mixture of lyricism and narrative. In some volumes the lyricism dominates, while in others the narrative is the focus. The early books, influenced obviously though not slavishly by Theodore Roethke and perhaps Hopkins, are infused with a sense of private anxiety and guilt. Both emotions are called forth most deeply by the memories of a brother who died before Dickey was born ('In the Tree House at Night') and his war experiences ('Drinking From a Helmet'). These early poems generally employ rhyme and metre.<br /><br />With Buckdancer's Choice, Dickey left traditional formalism behind, developing what he called a 'split-line' technique to vary the rhythm and look of the poem. Some critics argue that by doing so Dickey freed his true poetic voice. Others lament that the lack of formal device led to rhetorical, emotional, and intellectual excess. The truth probably lies somewhere between these two assessments, and it will be left to the reader to decide which phase of Dickey's career is most attractive.<br /><br />Dickey's most comprehensive volume is The Whole Motion (Hanover, NH, and London, 1992). His early poems are collected in The Early Motion (Middletown, 1981). Recent individual volumes include The Eagle's Mile (Hanover and London, 1990) and Falling, May Day Sermon, and Other Poems (Hanover and London, 1982). Dickey has also published collections of autobiographical essays, Self Interviews (Garden City, NY, 1970; repr. New York, 1984) and Sorties (Garden City, 197 1; repr. New York, 1984).<br /><br />From The Oxford Companion to Twentieth-century Poetry in English. Ed. Ian Hamilton. Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1994. Copyright © 1994 by Oxford University Press.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13963757699555948851" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
